  * Ottis and Needleman톩 ?픹, 318죇, Finally, The Pilgrim's progress occupies an
important place in the history of prose fiction. As Raleigh asserts, it is not
a novel, but is form and outline it bears the same relation to the novel proper,
as the Morality bears to the drama proper... Bunyan influenced the novel by his
direct narrative method, by writing for the middle class rather than for fine
gentlemen and by making his symbolical characters perfectly real and not frigidly
artificial.

땇톩 촦쥌 칊?랭톏 턿츃? 봺譏톩 좘돿 딞창멤램 탛츎톩 ?웥래 ?책봞.
쥌툃래 램뜬멤톋씉 딢?래 뺸빨 큙돘 떓래돝 ??톩 둧줭? 봞뉠촻톩 떓씉п 래엨닯빳봞.*
  * No man is capabe of translating poetry who, besides a genius to that art is
not a master both of his author's language, and of his own?
nor must we understand the language only of the poet but his particular
turn of thoughts and expressions, which are the character that distinguish
and as It were, individuate him from all other writers. When we come thus far tis
time. to look into ourselves, to conform our genius to his, to give his thought
(either the same turn, if our tongue will bear it, or, if
not, to vary the dress, not to alter or destroy the substance(Preface to his

  * Needleman & Otis톩 ?픹 339죇. The Acta diurna, posted daily in public place
by order of Julius Caesar, is probably the earthiest recorded effort to inform
citizens of current events. In Venice in 1562, began the posting of the Notizie
scritte, often called the Gazetta because that was the small fee charged readers
by the gevernment. News-sheets written by professinal teller writers were published
irregulary during the reign of Elizabeth. The first regular English paper was
Nathaniel Butler's the weekly Newes(1622~41) . During the Civil War many courant,
diurnals, and new-sheets were issued. Prominent were Roger L'Estrange the
intelligencer(1663); the london gazette(1666), the only serious attempt at
journalism during 17th century one controlled by the Under-Secretary of State,
John Dunton's athenian gazette(1690)

  * Legouis톩 ?픹 199죇. He replaces them by embroidered trimmings, by a narrative
made up of satire and sprightly reflections deeorated with mythological similes,

  What I speak, my fair Chloe, and what I write, shows
  The difference there Is betwix Nature and Art:
  I court others in verse, but I love thee in prose:
  And they have my whimsies, but thou hast my heart.

  * My Worthy friend sir roger when we are talking of the malice of parties very
frequently tells us an accident that happened to him when he was a sohool-boy
which was at the time when the feuds ran high between the Round-heads and
cavaliers. this worthy knight, being then but a stripling had occasion to
inquire which was the way to st. anne's lane? Upon which the person whom he
spoke to, instead of answering his question called him a young popish cur
and asked him who had made anne a saint? 'upon this' says sir roger I did
not think fit to repeat the former question, but going into every lane of
the neighourhood asked what they called the name of the Bane.1 By which
ingenious artifice he found out the place he inqnired after, without giving
offence to any party,

  * My reconcilement to the Yahoo-kind to
general might not be so difficult, if they would be content with those vices
and follies only which Nature hath entitled them to, I am not in the Beast
provoked at the sight of a lawyer, a pickpocket, a colonel, a fool, a lord,
a gamester, a politician, a whoremaster, a Physician, an evidence a suborner,
an attorney, a traitor, or the like; this is all according to the due course
of things: but when I behold a lump of deformity, and disceases both in body
and mind, smitten with pride, It immediatehy breaks all the measure, of my
patience; neither shall I be ever able to comprehend how such an animal and such a
vice could tally together,

  *틒; Through, grief and fondness in my breast rebel,
  When injur'd Thales bids the town farewell,
  Yet still my calmer thought his choice commend,
  I praise the hermit, but regret the friend,
  Resolv'd at lengh, from vice and London far,
  To breathein distant fields, a purer air,
  And, fix'd on Cambria's solitary shore
  Give to St. David one true Briton more.

  *틒; Daiches톩 ?픹 789 죇. the questions to be asked of a given poet are: What
kind of man, living in what age and circumstances, was he, and, being that sort of
man, with what degree of success did he produce works capable of giving
pleasure by their truth and liveliness? Liveliness, or novetly, it should be added,
was always a criterion insisted on by Johnson, as well as general truth.
  **틒; Lord Chesterfiold톩 쨦?킕 ⅰ릣 땇톩 п?톏 텮죟? 쥌툃래봞. Is not a patron,
my Lord, one who  looks with unconcern  on a man  struggling for life in  the waters, 
and,
when he has reached ground, encumbers him with help? The notice which you have
been pleased to take of any labours, had it been early, had been kind: but it has been
delayed till I am indifferent, and cannot enjoy it; till I am solitary, and cannot impart
it; till I am known, and do not want it. I hope it is on very cynical asperity, not to
confess obligations where no benefit has been received or to be unwilling that the public
should consider me as owing that, to a patron, which Providence has enabled me to
do for myself.

  James Boswell

  *틒; Legouis톩 ?픹 227죇. He had felt that the couplet, with its tendency to divide
up periods and to tender image fragmentary, did not allow him the spaciousness
which he desired and which his subject claimed. He borrowed therefore, from Milton
his long rhythmical periods, his varied cadence, and his way of carrying over sentences
from line to line.

  (Night, sable goddess.' from her ebon throne
  In rayless majesty, now stretches forth 
  Her leaden sceptre o'er a slumbering world
  Silence, how dead! and darkness, how profound!
  Nor eye, nor listening ear, an object finds;
  Creation sleeps. "Tis as the general pulse
  Of life stood still, and Nature made a pause,
  An awful praise! prophetic of her end.)

  (Now air is hushed, save where the weak-eyed bat,
  With short shrill shriek, flits by on leathern wing
  Or where the beetle winds
  His small but sullen horn,
  As oft he rises 'midst the twilight path,
  Against the pilgrim borne in heedless hum:
  Now teach me, maid composed,
  To breathe come softened strain.)
